Gooderham v. Adult & Family Services Div.


CA No. A22289


COURT OF APPEALS OF OREGON


64 Ore. App. 104; 667 P.2d 551; 1983 Ore. App. LEXIS 3260


March 11, 1983, Argued and submitted  
August 3, 1983 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Judicial Review from Adult and Family Services Division. Nos. 4-2801-0ZL798-1, 2-2801-OZJ759-3, 5-2801-MLI873-8, 1-2801-MXO597-0.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed in part; reversed in part; and remanded for further proceedings not inconsistent with this opinion.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner public aid recipients sought review of the final orders of respondent Adult and Family Services Division (AFSD) that terminated or reduced the special diet allowances that the recipients received pursuant to Or. Admin. R. 461-06-015.


OVERVIEW: The AFSD's decision to terminate or reduce the special diet allowances received by the recipients was based upon a temporary rule that amended Admin. R. 461-06-015. After the recipients sought judicial review, the AFSD adopted a permanent rule that made the criteria in the temporary rule retroactive. The court held that the retroactive application of the permanent rule was unreasonable in its prejudice to the recipients. Therefore, the court reversed that part of the AFSD's final order that terminated or reduced the allowances as of the effect date of the temporary rule. However, the court affirmed the termination of the allowances as of the effective date of the permanent rule. The court also remanded for recalculation of the amount of the special diet allowance to one recipient, finding that the permanent rule was invalid to the extent that it permitted utilization of food stamp allotments in determining the benefit level. The court concluded that the utilization of food stamp allotments was contrary to the purposes of 7 U.S.C.S. § 2017(b) of the Food Stamp Act, 7 U.S.C.S. § 2011 et seq., and Or. Rev. Stat. § 411.837.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the AFSD's final orders that terminated of the recipients allowances as of the effective date of the permanent rule. The final orders that terminated or reduced the allowances as of the effect date of the temporary rule were reversed. The court remanded for recalculation of the allowance to one recipient, that was calculated based upon his receipt of food stamps.

 

1171 of 1650 DOCUMENTS


Thede v. Polk County


CA No. A25566


COURT OF APPEALS OF OREGON


63 Ore. App. 738; 665 P.2d 1257; 1983 Ore. App. LEXIS 3034


May 9, 1983, Argued and submitted  
July 6, 1983 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Judicial Review from Land Use Board of Appeals. No. 82-049.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner citizens appealed an order of the Land Use Board of Appeals, which dismissed their appeal of a decision of respondent Polk County, Oregon, as untimely, under Or. Laws ch. 772, § 4(4) (1979), as amended by Or. Laws748, § 35 (1981). The notice of appeal was filed more than 30 days after the County's decision. The citizens urged that the late filing was excusable because they did not have notice of the County's hearing or decision.


OVERVIEW: The citizens claimed that the County's order was not final, although specific language of their notice of intent to appeal referred to the order as final. On appeal, the court held that (1) neither the Board not the court had authority to ignore the time limitation in ch. 772, § 4(4); (2) the language of the notice of intent to appeal was close to a judicial admission that the order was final; (3) the record before the Board did not establish the citizens' propositions concerning notice because statements that they relied on in a response to a motion to dismiss were not under oath; (4) the Board's only authority to supplement the record was provided in Or. Laws ch. 772, § 4(7) (1979), as amended by Or. Laws ch. 748, § 35 (1981), but the Board did not hold an evidentiary hearing as provided for therein, and the citizens did not assign such failure as error before the court; and (5) the Board's order of dismissal was proper because the notice of intent to appeal was, on its face, untimely, and no proper evidence was in the record even to create a question as to the citizens' right to appeal later than the statutes authorized.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment of the Board.

State v. Nossaman


CA No. A26449


COURT OF APPEALS OF OREGON


63 Ore. App. 789; 666 P.2d 1351; 1983 Ore. App. LEXIS 3045


May 16, 1983, Argued and submitted  
July 6, 1983 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from Circuit Court, Lane County. James R. Hargreaves, Judge. No. 10-81-11079.



DISPOSITION: Reversed and remanded for new trial.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant sought review of his conviction entered in the Circuit Court, Lane County (Oregon). Defendant was tried for unlawful possession on a dangerous weapon in violation of ORS 166.220, and for first-degree sodomy in violation of Or. Rev. Stat. § 163.405. He was convicted of the weapons charge.


OVERVIEW: Before defendant's trial, he moved to disqualify the trial judge. Although defendant testified that he had heard from other attorneys and from written sources that this judge was prejudiced against a certain class of criminal defendants of which he was a member. His motion for change of judge was nonetheless denied, and he was convicted of the weapons charge. The conviction was reversed and the case was remanded for a new trial. The court held that defendant's showing satisfied the requirements of Or. Rev. Stat. § 14.260 for a change of judge. While defendant's good faith could be challenged, the burden of good faith was satisfied by a showing that he received information that, if true, would reasonably lead him to fear the judge's prejudice. The court noted that, as a matter of public policy, it was better to substitute judges than make defendants prove the judge's prejudice. The court also held that the circuit court erred in instructing the jury that the carrying of a dangerous weapon was sufficient evidence of guilt, instead of that it was presumptive evidence of guilt. The instruction violated Or. Evid. Code 309 as regarded presumptions in jury instructions.


OUTCOME: Defendant's conviction was reversed and his case was remanded for a new trial.

Forman v. Clatsop County


CA No. A24527


COURT OF APPEALS OF OREGON


63 Ore. App. 617; 665 P.2d 365; 1983 Ore. App. LEXIS 3024


November 17, 1982, Argued and submitted; February 9, 1983, Resubmitted In Banc  
June 22, 1983 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Reconsideration Denied September 9, 1983. Petition for Review Allowed November 15, 1983 (296 Or App 56) See 297 Or 129, 681 P2d 786 (1984).


PRIOR HISTORY: Appeal from Circuit Court, Clatsop County. Delbert B. Mayer, Judge. No. CC-82-156.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiffs, neighboring property owners (neighbors), appealed a decision by the Circuit Court, Clatsop County (Oregon), which granted a motion to dismiss filed by defendant county on the ground that the circuit court lacked jurisdiction to hear the complaint because the Land Use Board of Appeals (LUBA) had exclusive jurisdiction to review the county's nonconforming use decision. Defendant purchasers were also named.


OVERVIEW: The purchasers bought a complex to use for recreational activities. The county's planning commission determined that the intended use was permitted under the then-existing zoning ordinance. The purchaser began development. However, two years later, the county adopted a new zoning ordinance that prohibited the intended use. The county's commission determined that the purchasers had a vested right to continue their nonconforming use. The neighbors appealed the county's resolution to LUBA, which dismissed the appeal because the circuit court had jurisdiction. The neighbors did not appeal LUBA's decision but filed a complaint in the circuit court, which dismissed for lack of jurisdiction. The court held that, under 1979 Or. Laws 772, § 4(1), LUBA was the only forum in which the neighbors could have contested the county's determination. Furthermore, there was no question that the decision was final and was made by a local government within the meaning of Or. Rev. Stat. § 197.015. Therefore, the neighbors failed to pursue their remedy, which was to appeal to the Court of Appeals of Oregon from the LUBA decision. That they failed to do.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the circuit court's dismissal of the action.

The circuit court dismissed her petition for writ of review challenging the decision. The officer's final decision that the owner had failed to establish a vested right and a nonconforming use was reviewable only by the Land Use Board of Appeals (Oregon), so the circuit court lacked subject matter jurisdiction properly dismissed the petition.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment of the circuit court.

Lent v. Employment Relations Bd.


CA No. A27426


COURT OF APPEALS OF OREGON


63 Ore. App. 400; 664 P.2d 1110; 1983 Ore. App. LEXIS 2933

June 8, 1983

PRIOR HISTORY: Appeal from Circuit Court, Marion County. Duane R. Ertsgaard, Judge. No. 136,338.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff Oregon Supreme Court Chief Justice sought review of a judgment of the Circuit Court, Marion County (Oregon), on a motion for judgment on the pleadings by defendants, Employment Relations Board and union, and intervenor union, that his exercise of personnel administration functions delegated by the Court Reform Act were subject to the provisions of the Public Employee Collective Bargaining Act (PECBA), Or. Rev. Stat. § 243.650 et seq.


OVERVIEW: The Chief Justice argued that his labor relations with employes of the Judicial Department were not subject to the PECBA because his authority under Or. Rev. Stat. § 1.002 and Or. Rev. Smithson v. Nordstrom, Inc.


CA No. A23326


COURT OF APPEALS OF OREGON


63 Ore. App. 423; 664 P.2d 1119; 1983 Ore. App. LEXIS 2937; 118 L.R.R.M. 3019


December 3, 1982, Argued and submitted  
June 8, 1983 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Nordstrom's Reconsideration Denied July 15, 1983. Smithson's Reconsideration Denied September 16, 1983. Petitions for Review Denied October 25, 1983 (295 Or 840).


PRIOR HISTORY: Appeal from Circuit Court, Multnomah County. Richard Unis, Judge. No. A8009-05210.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed in part; reversed in part, and remanded.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant, a former employer, sought review of the judgment in favor of plaintiff, a former employee, in the Circuit Court of Multnomah County (Oregon), in an action alleging claims for outrageous conduct, defamation, and a breach of employment contract for unpaid wages.


OVERVIEW: The employee, a former saleswomen in a department store, filed suit against her employer for outrageous conduct, defamation, and breach of contract, all stemming from an interview in which she was questioned by store security personnel about a suspected theft. On appeal from a verdict for the employee, the court affirmed in part, noting that the employer's assignments of error on the defamation and wage claims were without merit. Further, the court noted that the jury could find that the employee was threatened with criminal prosecution if she did not sign a confession and could infer that the security officers did not reasonably believe that sufficient evidence existed to charge her with theft. If so, the jury could determine that such conduct, combined with other aspects of the interrogation, exceeded the bounds of socially tolerable employer practices. There was, thus, sufficient evidence to submit the outrageous conduct claim to the jury. However, the court reversed and remanded for a new trial on the outrageous conduct claim when the jury instruction encompassed both actionable and non-actionable conduct and authorized a verdict for conduct that was socially acceptable.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment for the employee on her defamation and wage claims. The court reversed the judgment for the employee on her outrageous conduct claim and remanded for a new trial on this claim.

 

1177 of 1650 DOCUMENTS


O'Brien v. Eugene Chemical Exports, Inc.


CA Nos. A25193, A25194 (Cases Consolidated)


COURT OF APPEALS OF OREGON


63 Ore. App. 284; 664 P.2d 1106; 1983 Ore. App. LEXIS 2811


March 2, 1983, Argued and submitted  
May 25, 1983 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from Circuit Court, Linn County. Wendell H. Tompkins, Judge. Nos. 80-2471, 80-2512.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendants, owner and employee, sought review of a judgment from the Circuit Court, Linn County (Oregon), which granted plaintiffs, mother and son, general and punitive damages for false arrest. The owner and the employee claimed that the mother and the son were guilty of criminal trespass "as a matter of law" and that the employee had probable cause to make a citizen's arrest.


OVERVIEW: The mother and the son were picking berries on the owner's property. The father formerly worked for the owner. The mother and the son knew that there was a "No Trespassing" sign. The employee made a citizen's arrest and the mother and the son were arrested but the prosecution was dropped. The mother and the son brought an action against the owner and the employee for false arrest and were awarded general and punitive damages in a jury trial. The owner and the employee sought review. The court held that: (1) proof that the arrest was lawful was a defense; (2) pursuant to Or. Rev. Stat. § 133.225(1), a citizen's arrest cannot be made based on probable cause; (3) pursuant to Or. Rev. Stat. § 164.245(1), entry or unlawfully remaining, as defined in Or. Rev. Stat. § 164.205(3)(a), in or upon premises constituted criminal trespass in the second degree; (4) pursuant to Or. Rev. Stat. § 164.205(4), the premises were open to the public if a reasonable person would have believed that no permission to enter or remain was required; and (5) there was evidence to support the verdict.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment that awarded the mother and son general and punitive damages against the owner and the employee for false arrest.

Planned Parenthood Asso. v. Department of Human Resources


CA No. A20856


COURT OF APPEALS OF OREGON


63 Ore. App. 41; 663 P.2d 1247; 1983 Ore. App. LEXIS 2798


December 23, 1981, Argued and submitted  
May 11, 1983 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Reconsideration Denied July 15, 1983. Petition for Review Allowed August 16, 1983 (295 Or 541) See 297 Or 562, 687 P2d 785 (1984).


PRIOR HISTORY: Judicial Review for determination of validity of a rule from Department of Human Resources.



DISPOSITION: Rule held invalid.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioners, individuals and public interest organizations, brought a proceeding pursuant to Or. Rev. Stat. § 183.400 to invalidate an administrative rule promulgated by respondent Department of Human Resources. The rule, Or. Admin. R. 461-14-052, provided state medical assistance for certain "elective" abortions.


OVERVIEW: The department promulgated Or. Admin. R. 461-14-052, which provided state medical assistance for certain "elective" abortions. The rule was challenged on grounds that it violated an indigent woman's right of privacy and religious freedom under Or. Const. art. I, §§ 1, 3, 9, 33, and the Equal Privileges and Immunities Clause under Or. Const. art. I, § 20. The challenge was to the failure to fund any surgical procedure that a physician determined to be medically necessary, but not life-threatening, if it involved an abortion. The court held that the rule was invalid under Or. Const. art. I, § 20. The court concluded that there was a class of women who were denied a privilege available to other class members on equal terms. The court applied a balancing test which weighed the detriment to the affected class members against the state's justification for the disparate treatment. The rule denied benefits for pregnancy-related medically necessary services that involved abortion solely on that ground. The court concluded that the state's fiscal interest in limiting funding for abortions and it interest in protecting potential life did not outweigh the woman's interest in her health.


OUTCOME: The court held the rule, which provided state medical assistance for certain "elective" abortions, invalid. The court concluded that the rule violated the Privileges and Immunities Clause of the Oregon Constitution.

 

1179 of 1650 DOCUMENTS


State v. Hacker


CA No. A25684


COURT OF APPEALS OF OREGON


62 Ore. App. 691; 662 P.2d 21; 1983 Ore. App. LEXIS 2574


January 12, 1983, Argued and submitted  
April 27, 1983 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from Circuit Court, Lane County. F. Gordon Cottrell, Judge. No. 10-79-11713.



DISPOSITION: Reversed and remanded for trial.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The State sought review of a decision of the Circuit Court, Lane County (Oregon), which granted defendant's motion to dismiss the indictment against her for first degree forgery and first degree theft for lack of a speedy trial.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was indicted for first degree forgery and first degree theft. The trial court later granted defendant's motion to dismiss the indictment for lack of a speedy trial. On appeal, the court reversed and remanded. The delay in the case was neither unreasonable under Or. Rev. Stat. § 135.747 nor unconstitutional. The delay before defendant's motion to dismiss was just over two and one half years. Slightly over a year of this period was due to the State's appeal when the trial court granted defendant's motion to suppress certain evidence. Defendant alleged no bad faith on the part of the State in seeking the appeal. Defendant had to assume some responsibility for the delay attributable to the trial court's failure to reconsider the motion to suppress in time for the new trial date. Defendant made her first motion to dismiss for lack of speedy trial two weeks before her trial date, over two years after the indictment. Defendant could have asserted her right much earlier than that. Further, defendant alleged no specific prejudice but relied on the length of the delay. Defendant was not incarcerated while awaiting trial and did not claim her defense was hampered by the delay.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the decision of the trial court and remanded the action for trial.

 

1180 of 1650 DOCUMENTS


State v. Holmes


CA Nos. A21658, A21659, A21660 (Cases Consolidated)


COURT OF APPEALS OF OREGON


62 Ore. App. 652; 661 P.2d 556; 1983 Ore. App. LEXIS 2545


May 14, 1982, Argued and submitted  
April 13, 1983 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Reconsideration Denied May 27, 1983. Petition for Review Denied July 6, 1983 (295 Or 297).


PRIOR HISTORY: Appeal from Circuit Court, Multnomah County. Robert P. Jones, Judge. Nos. C80-07-32567 (Control), C80-07-32569, C80-07-32571.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant sought review of a decision from the Circuit Court, Multnomah County (Oregon), which entered two convictions against defendant for robbery in the first degree and a conviction for attempted murder. The trial court found that defendant was a dangerous offender and sentenced him on each conviction to a maximum term of 30 years, with a minimum term of 15 years.


OVERVIEW: Defendant contended that a minimum term imposed under Or. Rev. Stat. § 144.110 was limited by the maximum indeterminate sentence provided for the underlying felony and that in his case the 15-year minimum terms exceeded the 10-year maximum authorized by Or. Rev. Stat. § 144.110(1) for conviction of a Class A felony. The court noted that the length of the minimum term was limited by the sentence imposed and not by the maximum indeterminate term provided for the underlying felony. As defendant was convicted of three Class A felonies, the trial court was authorized to impose a maximum 30-year sentence on each conviction. The 15-year minimum terms were expressly authorized by Or. Rev. Stat. § 144.110(1). The court also denied the claim that the 15-year minimum terms were unconstitutional because they were disproportionate to the 10-year minimum term allowable for the completed act of murder. A sentence of up to 15 years for murder was permissible under Or. Rev. Stat. § 110. Defendant's sentences were not disproportionate because the 15-year minimum terms imposed did not exceed the maximum minimum sentence authorized for murder under Or. Rev. Stat. § 144.110.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment convicting and sentencing defendant.

State v. Garcias

1182 of 1650 DOCUMENTS


State v. Way


CA No. A24046


COURT OF APPEALS OF OREGON


62 Ore. App. 459; 660 P.2d 1087; 1983 Ore. App. LEXIS 2483


September 17, 1982, Argued and submitted  
March 30, 1983 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Reconsideration Denied April 14, 1983. Petition for Review Denied May 17, 1983 (295 Or 31).


PRIOR HISTORY: Appeal from Circuit Court, Jackson County. L. A. Merryman, Judge. No. 80-4105-C-2.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant challenged a judgment of the Circuit Court, Jackson County (Oregon), which imposed a 10-year minimum sentence after he was convicted of murder. Defendant alleged that a 1978 amendment to Or. Rev. Stat. § 163.115(5), which required a 25-year mandatory minimum sentence for murder, repealed, by implication, Or. Rev. Stat. § 144.110(1) as applied to murder, and that § 144.110(1) was not revived by the repeal of Or. Rev. Stat. § 163.115(5).


OVERVIEW: By initiative, the people of Oregon amended Or. Rev. Stat. § 163.115(5) to provide for a 25 year minimum sentence for a murder conviction. The statute was held unconstitutional. The court affirmed defendant's sentence and held as follows: (1) there is not such repugnance between the statutes that Or. Rev. Stat. § 144.110(1) was repealed by implication because § 144.110(1) applied to all felony cases and allowed, but did not require, a judge to impose a minimum term of up to one-half of the sentence imposed; (2) Or. Rev. Stat. § 163.115(5), as amended, applied only to murder and on its face purported neither to be a substitute for Or. Rev. Stat. § 144.110(1) nor to preclude the simultaneous validity of both statutes; (3) when Or. Rev. Stat. § 163.115(5), the more specific statute, was found unconstitutional, Or. Rev. Stat. § 144.110(1) was no longer controlled by it and applied by its terms to "any felony," including murder; and (4) the 1981 legislature did not intend that there be no minimum term for murder following the repeal of the 1978 measure.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment.

 

1183 of 1650 DOCUMENTS


In re Johnson


CA No. A25244


COURT OF APPEALS OF OREGON


62 Ore. App. 386; 660 P.2d 710; 1983 Ore. App. LEXIS 2465


January 19, 1983, Argued and submitted  
March 23, 1983 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from Circuit Court, Deschutes County. George W. Neilson, Judge Pro Tempore. No. 30754.



DISPOSITION: Reversed and remanded.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant owners sought review of an order of the Circuit Court, Deschutes County (Oregon), which dismissed their appeal from an order of respondent county board of commissioners, establishing a way of necessity across the owners' land and fixing the amount of compensation therefor. The circuit court dismissed the appeal on the ground the owners should have petitioned the circuit court for a writ of review under Or. Rev. Stat. § 34.010 et seq.


OVERVIEW: The owners appealed to the circuit court pursuant to Or. Rev. Stat. § 376.175(4), which provided that any party to the action for a way of necessity could contest the order of the county governing body in an appeal filed with the circuit court. The board claimed that review of its order was exclusively by writ of review. It relied on Or. Rev. Stat. § 203.113, which provided that the decisions of a county court made in the transaction of county business could be reviewed only as provided in Or. Rev. Stat. §§ 34.010 to 34.100, and on Or. Rev. Stat. § 34.020, which provided that any party to any proceeding before any inferior court, officer, or tribunal could have the decision thereof reviewed for errors, only as provided in Or. Rev. Stat. §§ 34.010 to 34.100. The court reversed the circuit court. State laws gave separate treatment to proceedings respecting ways of necessity. Further, since 1919, a landowner could contest an order of a county board, establishing a way of necessity, by appeal to the circuit court. Thus, it held that, as provided in Or. Rev. Stat. § 376.175(4), an order of the board in a proceeding to establish a way of necessity could be appealed to the circuit court.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the circuit court's judgment and remanded.

 

1184 of 1650 DOCUMENTS


State v. Wiemals


CA No. A23820


COURT OF APPEALS OF OREGON


62 Ore. App. 266; 660 P.2d 702; 1983 Ore. App. LEXIS 2427


July 9, 1982, Argued and submitted  
March 16, 1983 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from District Court, Linn County. Rick J. McCormick, Judge. No. 8101-0018M.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed an order of the District Court, Linn County (Oregon), which convicted him of issuing a false financial statement in violation of Or. Rev. Stat. § 165.100. Defendant applied for a loan for his corporation from a bank and entered into a security agreement on the corporation's behalf, in which he stated that it owned seven vehicles. The jury found that defendant had sold one of the vehicles before he signed the agreement.


OVERVIEW: At trial, defendant moved for judgment of acquittal on the grounds that the security agreement was not a "financial statement," within the meaning of § 165.100. He also moved for an order arresting judgment on the grounds that § 165.100 was unconstitutionally vague. The trial court denied these motions. On appeal, the court held that (1) Or. Rev. Stat. § 165.100 replaced former Or. Rev. Stat. §§ 165.615 and 165.620, and the legislative history showed that there was no intent to change the former law; (2) the provisions of the former law clearly showed, in the context of an extension of credit, the term "financial statement" included any written statement purporting to describe a corporation's financial condition or ability to pay and on which the declarant intended the lender to rely; (3) the security agreement was a financial statement, within the meaning of the law, because it described the corporation's ability to pay, and the bank relied on it; and (4) Or. Rev. Stat. § 165.100 was not unconstitutionally because it fairly informed persons of common intelligence, courts, or juries, of what types of statements were included in the term "financial statements."


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment of the trial court.

 

1185 of 1650 DOCUMENTS


1000 Friends of Oregon v. Wasco County Court


CA No. A24016


COURT OF APPEALS OF OREGON


62 Ore. App. 75; 659 P.2d 1001; 1983 Ore. App. LEXIS 2407


June 2, 1982, Argued and submitted  
March 2, 1983 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Reconsideration Denied April 14, 1983. Petition for Review Denied June 29, 1983 (295 Or 259).


PRIOR HISTORY: Judicial Review from Land Use Board of Appeals. No. 81-132.



DISPOSITION: Reversed and remanded.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioners sought review of a Land Use Board of Appeals (LUBA) order, which dismissed their appeal for lack of jurisdiction. Petitioners had challenged an order of respondent, the Wasco County Court, which granted a petition to incorporate the City of Rajneeshpuram, fixing boundaries of the proposed city and setting a date for a special election on the matter of incorporation.


OVERVIEW: LUBA held that the county court's order was not a "land use decision" under Or. Rev. Stat. § 197.015(10) and was therefore not subject to LUBA review. Petitioners asserted that it was a land use decision. The court reversed the dismissal, holding that the county's decision to authorize an incorporation election was a land use decision subject to LUBA review. Land use decisions subject to LUBA review included local planning activities that would have a significant impact on present or future land use. The standard meant that if the completed process would have a significant impact, the decision to implement the process was reviewable by LUBA. The implementation of the county's decision entailed the holding of an election to incorporate, to set urban boundaries, and to effect a transition from rural to urban land use. Therefore, it would have a significant impact if, as appeared reasonably likely, the voters did elect to incorporate. If they did so, land that could not have been used for urban use before would be available for future urban use.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the dismissal of petitioners' appeal and remanded the case.

 

1186 of 1650 DOCUMENTS


State v. Sparklin


CA No. A21337


COURT OF APPEALS OF OREGON


61 Ore. App. 608; 658 P.2d 571; 1983 Ore. App. LEXIS 2238


March 15, 1982, Argued and submitted  
February 16, 1983 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Reconsideration Denied April 1, 1983. Petition for Review Allowed May 3, 1983 (294 Or 792) See 296 Or 85, 672 P2d 1182 (1983).


PRIOR HISTORY: Appeal from Circuit Court, Multnomah County. William M. Dale, Judge. No. C80-12-34330.



DISPOSITION: Convictions affirmed; sentence for felony murder conviction vacated and remanded for resentencing.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed a judgment of the Circuit Court, Multnomah County (Oregon), which convicted him of manslaughter in the first degree, felony murder, and robbery in the first degree.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was arrested and jailed in Eugene for forgery. He was arraigned the following day and requested appointed counsel. The Portland police interviewed defendant as a suspect in a homicide that was not related to the forgery charge. Defendant gave the Portland police a series of incriminating statements. He was convicted of manslaughter in the first degree, felony murder, and robbery in the first degree. On appeal, he argued that after he had requested appointed counsel at arraignment, the police could not initiate any interrogation of him. The court held that the challenged statements were admissible. Defendant demurred to the felony murder indictment on the ground that Or. Rev. Stat. § 163.115(1)(b) was unconstitutional. The court held that the trial court did not err in overruling the demurrer because there was not such a disparity in culpability between felony murder and intentional murder that the state or federal constitutions required different levels of punishment. The trial court did err in imposing a minimum sentence of 25 years on the felony murder conviction.


OUTCOME: The convictions were affirmed, but the sentence for the felony murder charge was vacated and remanded for resentencing.

 

1187 of 1650 DOCUMENTS


State v. Boyce


CA No. A25625


COURT OF APPEALS OF OREGON


61 Ore. App. 662; 658 P.2d 577; 1983 Ore. App. LEXIS 2242


January 11, 1983, Argued and submitted  
February 16, 1983 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Reconsideration Denied April 1, 1983. Petition for Review Denied May 24, 1983 (295 Or 122).


PRIOR HISTORY: Appeal from District Court, Multnomah County. Linda L. Bergman, Judge. No. 225173.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed his conviction by the District Court, Multnomah County (Oregon) for carrying a loaded firearm on the ground that Portland, Or., Ordinance No. 138210, § 14.32.010, the ordinance under which he was convicted, violated Or. Const. art I, § 27, which related to the right to bear arms.


OVERVIEW: A city police officer noticed defendant's car traveling at a high rate of speed without headlights. The officer stopped the car and as he approached it, he observed what appeared to be a handgun on the seat and found it was loaded after he picked it up. Defendant was arrested and convicted. Defendant contended that Portland, Or., Ordinance No. 138210, § 14.32.010 was unconstitutional and relied upon case law in which the possession of billy clubs was found to be constitutionally protected. In affirming the convictions, the court held that the statute challenged in the case law, Or. Rev. Stat. § 166.510(1) and the ordinance were fundamentally different. The statute's proscription of the mere possession of certain weapons made it unconstitutional. The ordinance, however, did not proscribe mere possession of even a loaded firearm, but made criminal possession of a loaded gun in a public place. Such an encroachment on a constitutional right was permissible when the unrestricted exercise of the right posed a clear threat to the interests and welfare of the public in general. The means chosen by the city to protect its citizens did not unreasonably interfere with the right.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the trial court's decision.

 

1188 of 1650 DOCUMENTS


Salem College & Academy, Inc. v. Employment Div.


CA No. A20465


COURT OF APPEALS OF OREGON


61 Ore. App. 616; 659 P.2d 415; 1983 Ore. App. LEXIS 2244


September 28, 1981, Argued and submitted  
February 16, 1983 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Employment Division's Reconsideration Denied April 29, 1983. Salem Academy's Reconsideration Denied July 15, 1983. Employment Division's Petition for Review Allowed July 6, 1983 (295 Or 297). Salem Academy's Petition for Review Denied August 16, 1983 (295 Or 541).


PRIOR HISTORY: Judicial Review from Employment Division. No. 80-T-56.



DISPOSITION: Reversed; remanded with instructions to quash the Notice of Assessment and for such further action as may be necessary and not inconsistent with this opinion.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner school sought review of a decision from the referee of respondent employment division, which affirmed the Division's tax assessment for reimbursement of unemployment compensation paid to four of the school's former employees. The school claimed it was statutorily exempt from the Unemployment Compensation Act, Or. Rev. Stat. 657.005-657.990 and that the application of the Act was forbidden by the U.S. Constitution.


OVERVIEW: The Division assessed taxes against the school for reimbursement of unemployment compensation paid to four of the school's former teachers. The school filed an appeal. In reversing and remanding the decision, the court held that as an independent religious school, the school was exempt from the Act because the application of it violated the Establishment Clause. The court determined that the remedy of subjecting church schools to the Act directly contradicted Or. Rev. Stat. § 657.072(1) but also found that § 657.072(1) improperly favored one organizational form of religious expression by singling out schools that were linked to established churches. The court stated that Congress intended to exempt church-operated schools from the Act and likely would expand the exemption to apply to religious schools. The court reasoned that by requiring a school to be under the control of a church with the power to determine the school's doctrine in order to be exempt under § 657.072, the Act infringed on citizens' rights to develop their own beliefs and discouraged a variety of sects. The court concluded the exemption could not be conditioned on whether a religious school was linked to a church.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the Division's decision and remanded the case with instructions to quash the notice of assessment.

 

1189 of 1650 DOCUMENTS


State v. Mains


CA No. A24630


COURT OF APPEALS OF OREGON


61 Ore. App. 422; 657 P.2d 220; 1983 Ore. App. LEXIS 2150


September 13, 1982, Argued and submitted; December 22, 1982, Resubmitted In Banc  
January 19, 1983 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Reconsideration Denied March 11, 1983. Petition for Review Allowed April 12, 1983 (294 Or 749) See 295 Or 640, 669 P2d 1112 (1983).


PRIOR HISTORY: Appeal from Circuit Court, Lane County. Edwin E. Allen, Judge. No. 74-0191.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: After post-conviction relief because of inadequate assistance of appellate counsel, defendant appealed his conviction for murder in the Circuit Court, Lane County (Oregon). Defendant argued that he was improperly denied his Miranda warnings prior to examination by the State's psychiatrist, that jury instructions were erroneous, that his motion for a mistrial was improperly denied, and that he was denied due process.


OVERVIEW: Before his trial for the murder of a 15-month-old child, defendant obtained an order authorizing an examination by a psychiatrist at public expense to determine his mental state at the time of the murder. After a second motion, he was given another examination at public expense, and he gave notice of his intent to introduce evidence at trial on the issue of criminal responsibility. The State obtained an order requiring defendant to submit to a psychiatric examination. The order provided that defendant's attorney could be present. Defendant was not read his Miranda rights by the State's psychiatrist. Defendant was convicted, and he appealed. The court affirmed, holding that defendant knew that the results of the examination could be used in court. Thus, there was no error in the psychiatrist's examination. Because the instructions as a whole did not improperly shift the burden of proof to defendant or impermissibly comment on defendant's failure to testify, the trial court's instruction on weaker and less satisfactory evidence was harmless beyond a reasonable doubt. The trial court did not err in ruling on defendant's motion for a mistrial, and defendant was not denied due process.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed defendant's conviction for murder.

 

1190 of 1650 DOCUMENTS


Circuit Court of Oregon, Fifteenth Judicial Dist., etc. v. AFSCME, Local 502-A


CA No. A23894


COURT OF APPEALS OF OREGON


61 Ore. App. 311; 657 P.2d 1237; 1983 Ore. App. LEXIS 2164; 115 L.R.R.M. 2168


September 13, 1982, Argued and submitted; December 9, 1982, Resubmitted In Banc  
January 19, 1983 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Reconsideration Denied March 11, 1983. Petition for Review Allowed April 12, 1983 (294 Or 749) See 295 Or 542, 669 P2d 314 (1983).


PRIOR HISTORY: Judicial Review from Employment Relations Board. No. C-65-81.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner juvenile court judge sought review of the order of the Oregon Employment Relations Board (ERB), which held that the judge committed unfair labor practices under Or. Rev. Stat. § 243.672(1)(e), (g) by refusing to bargain with respondent union, the exclusive representative of court counselors, and by establishing terms and conditions of employment for the counselors at variance with the terms of a collective bargaining agreement.


OVERVIEW: Pursuant to Or. Rev. Stat. § 419.604(1), juvenile court counselors served at the pleasure of and at a salary designated by the judge. The ERB found that the judge committed unfair labor practices under Or. Rev. Stat. § 243.672(1)(e), (g) by refusing to bargain with the union and by establishing terms and conditions of employment for the counselors at variance with the terms of a collective bargaining agreement. The judge challenged the ERB's order, claiming that he and the employees of the juvenile court were not subject to the Public Employe Collective Bargaining Act (PECBA), Or. Rev. Stat. § 243.650 et seq. On appeal, the court affirmed. The court held that Or. Rev. Stat. § 419.604(1) did not preclude application of the PECBA to the judge and the counselors because the statutes were not inconsistent. Further, applying the bargaining and terms and conditions provisions of the PECBA to the judge and the counselors was not a separation of powers violation under Or. Const. art. III, § 1, because the provisions did not unduly burden or interfere with judicial functions. However, the court declined to address the issue of whether the PECBA, taken as a whole, could apply to any court.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the decision holding that the judge committed unfair labor practices by refusing to bargain with the union and by establishing terms and conditions of employment for the counselors at variance with the terms of the collective bargaining agreement.

 

1191 of 1650 DOCUMENTS


Portland v. White


CA No. A22881


COURT OF APPEALS OF OREGON


61 Ore. App. 120; 655 P.2d 629; 1982 Ore. App. LEXIS 4274


April 12, 1982, Argued and submitted  
December 22, 1982 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from District Court, Multnomah County. William C. Snouffer, Judge. DA No. 216212-8107.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant challenged the decision of the District Court, Multnomah County (Oregon), which convicted defendant of violating Portland, Or., City Code § 14.68.105, which made it unlawful for any person to promote, engage in, or profit from gambling in any form except a social game.


OVERVIEW: A police officer testified that he had observed defendant and others playing dice for money in a public park. On seeing the officer, defendant ran. A pair of dice was found in his pocket when he was caught and arrested. Defendant contended that the ordinance was unconstitutionally vague because it did not define "gambling." The court held that the failure of an ordinance to define a term used therein called for the application of its ordinary meaning. The ordinary meaning of the term "gambling" was the act or practice of betting, the act of playing a game and consciously risking money or other stakes on its outcome, or the act of risking something on an uncertain event. The court concluded that the ordinance was not unconstitutionally vague because those to whom the ordinance was addressed could discern from the ordinary meaning of the term "gambling" what conduct the city did or did not intend to include in the prohibition.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed.

 

1192 of 1650 DOCUMENTS


In re Adoption of Gibson


CA No. A22688


COURT OF APPEALS OF OREGON


60 Ore. App. 768; 655 P.2d 604; 1982 Ore. App. LEXIS 4137


May 28, 1982, Argued and submitted  
December 15, 1982 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Reconsideration Denied January 21, 1983. Petition for Review Allowed March 1, 1983 (294 Or 569) See 295 Or 112, 663 P2d 769 (1983).


PRIOR HISTORY: Appeal from Circuit Court, Benton County. Richard Mengler, Judge. No. 1181.



DISPOSITION: Reversed and remanded.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellants, mother and her new husband, sought review of the judgment of the Circuit Court, Benton County (Oregon), which ruled in favor of respondent biological father in appellants' adoption proceeding in which they contended that the father's consent was not required because he had either wilfully deserted his two minor children or wilfully neglected them without just and sufficient cause within the meaning of Or. Rev. Stat. § 109.324.


OVERVIEW: The mother and biological father were divorced, and the mother was awarded custody of their minor son and unborn daughter. The mother moved to another state without providing her new address to father, but he learned of it from a third party. After the divorce the father saw the son only once and never asked to see the daughter. When the mother's new husband sought to adopt the children, the father made one child support payment. The mother and her new husband argued that the father's consent to the adoption was not required because the father had either deserted or neglected the children for more than a year. The trial court ruled in favor of the father and appellants sought review. The court noted that desertion in the context of § 109.324 required proof that a parent had voluntarily foregone exercise of all parental rights. The court found that the father had deserted the daughter within the meaning of the statute. The court found further that the father had neglected the son within the meaning of the statute in failing to provide for his support, stating that the father's reliance on bad advice from counsel did not relieve him of his support obligations.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the judgment that the trial court had granted in favor of the father. The court remanded for further proceedings so that the trial court, in the exercise of its discretion, could ascertain whether the father's consent was required for the adoption of his children by the mother's new husband.

 

1193 of 1650 DOCUMENTS


State v. Gortmaker


CA Nos. 19226, 19227, 19228 (cases consolidated)


COURT OF APPEALS OF OREGON


60 Ore. App. 723; 655 P.2d 575; 1982 Ore. App. LEXIS 4147


November 20, 1981, Argued and submitted; November 3, 1982, Resubmitted In Banc  
December 15, 1982 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Reconsideration Denied February 9, 1983. Petition for Review Allowed March 8, 1983 (294 Or 569) See 295 Or 505, 668 P2d 354 (1983).


PRIOR HISTORY: Appeal from the Circuit Court, Marion County. Roland K. Rodman, Judge Pro Tempore. Nos. 119905, 119906, 119907.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed the judgment of the Circuit Court, Marion County (Oregon), which convicted defendant of theft in the first degree, tampering with public records, unsworn falsification, and official misconduct.


OVERVIEW: Ten persons were drawn for the special grand jury, but five were eliminated on the sole authority of the court administrator. The trial court failed to address whether the grand jury was properly chosen because it determined that Or. Rev. Stat. § 135.510 barred defendant from challenging his indictment. Defendant argued that the trial court erred in failing to quash the indictment on the ground that the grand jury was selected in violation of Or. Const. art. VII, § 5(2) and in denying his motions for judgment of acquittal on each of the crimes charged. The court found that § 135.510 was not a bar to defendant's motion to quash the indictment. The court determined that the method employed to select the grand jury violated § 5(2). However, the court concluded that since defendant was convicted by a fair trial, there was no persuasive reasons to overturn defendant's reliable conviction. The court further found sufficient evidence to support each of the crimes charged.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed defendant's convictions for theft in the first degree, tampering with public records, unsworn falsification, and official misconduct.

 

1194 of 1650 DOCUMENTS


In re Application of Angus


CA No. A25245


COURT OF APPEALS OF OREGON


60 Ore. App. 546; 655 P.2d 208; 1982 Ore. App. LEXIS 4116


September 21, 1982, Argued and submitted  
December 8, 1982 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Reconsideration Denied January 21, 1983. Petition for Review Denied March 1, 1983 (294 Or 569).


PRIOR HISTORY: Appeal from Circuit Court, Multnomah County. Kathleen B. Nachtigal, Judge. No. D8204-68177.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant adoptive parents sought review of a habeas corpus decision of the Circuit Court, Multnomah County (Oregon) awarding custody of a one-year old child to plaintiff natural parents under the Indian Child Welfare Act of 1978 (ICWA), 25 U.S.C.S. § 1901 et seq.


OVERVIEW: The natural parents, who were members of Indian tribes, brought a habeas corpus action seeking the release of their one-year old child from the adoptive parents. The trial court held in favor of the natural parents and the court affirmed. The court held that the trial court did not err in allowing the natural parents to reopen their case because this was a case in which the interests of a child were at stake. The court also held that the broad remedial purposes of the ICWA supported the trial court's decision to grant the motion to reopen. The court further held that there was sufficient evidence to support the natural parents' contention that they were members of Indian tribes. As to the constitutionality of the ICWA, the court held that the protection of the integrity of Indian families was a permissible goal that was rationally tied to the fulfillment of congress's unique guardianship obligation toward the Indians and that the ICWA was, therefore, not unconstitutional.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the trial court's granting of the natural parents petition for habeas corpus.

 

1195 of 1650 DOCUMENTS


Elliott v. Oregon International Mining Co.


CA No. A23405


COURT OF APPEALS OF OREGON


60 Ore. App. 474; 654 P.2d 663; 1982 Ore. App. LEXIS 4129


August 4, 1982, Argued and submitted  
December 1, 1982 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from Circuit Court, Grant County. Thomas M. Mosgrove, Judge. No. 7844.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff patent owners challenged the order of the Circuit Court of Grant County, which granted defendant mining company's motion for summary judgment and dismissed the owners' complaint for general and punitive damages for damaging and destroying the surface, trees, and fences on the owners' property.


OVERVIEW: The owners' patent contained a mineral reservation in favor of the United States. The company obtained its mineral rights through federal legislation. The trial court found that the government retained a reversionary interest in the minerals not extracted by company and that the county could neither prohibit the government from regulating its property nor enforce zoning on property in federal ownership. The owners argued that the county ordinances were constitutional. The court affirmed the judgment because the owners' complaint coupled with the company's answer, motion for summary judgment, and affidavit, did not raise any issue of material fact. The only issue properly before the trial court was a matter of law: whether the county ordinances impermissibly conflicted with federal regulations of reserved mining rights under the Stock Raising Homestead Act. The court held that the county ordinances did not supplement federal mining law. Rather, they completely prohibited a mineral claimant from conducting any surface mining on patented land. The county was unable to prohibit conduct, which Congress had specifically authorized.


OUTCOME: Summary judgment in favor of the mining company was affirmed.

 

1196 of 1650 DOCUMENTS


State v. Bowman


CA No. A22425


COURT OF APPEALS OF OREGON


60 Ore. App. 184; 653 P.2d 254; 1982 Ore. App. LEXIS 3800


April 26, 1982, Submitted on record and briefs  
November 10, 1982 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from District Court, Josephine County. Michael Mowrey, Judge. No. 81-106-M.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant sought review of a judgment of the Josephine County District Court (Oregon), which convicted her of the offense of failure to send her child to school under Or. Rev. Stat. §§ 339.020, 339.990.


OVERVIEW: While living in one school district, defendant taught her children at home with the permission of the superintendent. Defendant and her children moved to a different school district, and she requested to teach her younger child at home. Defendant and the superintendent of the new school district had a conflict, and defendant refused to present the child for testing. The superintendent revoked the permission for home teaching, and defendant did not enroll or register the child as required. Defendant was convicted of failing to send her child to school under Or. Rev. Stat. §§ 339.020, 339.990, and the court affirmed the conviction. The court held that Or. Rev. Stat. § 339.030(2), (6), was not unconstitutionally vague. The court found that, although children taught in private school and children taught by a parent were treated differently, the difference in treatment was not an improper legislative classification. The court further found no improper delegation in the statutory requirements that the state Board of Education prepare examinations for children taught by a parent and that the local superintendent determine whether the child was being properly taught.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the trial court's judgment against defendant.

 

1197 of 1650 DOCUMENTS


State v. Rodinsky


CA No. A22618


COURT OF APPEALS OF OREGON


60 Ore. App. 193; 653 P.2d 551; 1982 Ore. App. LEXIS 3801


April 26, 1982, Argued and submitted  
November 10, 1982 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from District Court, Multnomah County. Berkeley A. Smith, Judge. No. M066944.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant challenged her conviction from the District Court, Multnomah County (Oregon) for failure to obey a police officer in violation of Or. Rev. Stat. § 487.100, a Class C traffic infraction. Defendant's motion for a judgment of acquittal was denied. Defendant contended that the initial stop of her vehicle was unlawful and that any subsequent order of the police officer was, therefore, unlawful, and that the statute was void for vagueness.


OVERVIEW: Although the police officer told defendant to remain in her vehicle while he ran a radio check on her license, defendant came to the police vehicle and became loud and abusive. After multiple requests for defendant to return to her vehicle, the police officer radioed for backup and defendant was handcuffed and cited. The court opined that even if the stop was unlawful, the police officer had authority to order defendant to return to her vehicle. The court pointed out that the statute encompassed only orders under the police officer's general authority to direct, control, or regulate traffic, so that the statute did not subject citizens to unbridled power and discretion. Defendant's vehicle and the police vehicle were stopped in a lane of traffic and constituted an impediment to normal traffic flow as long as they remained. The police officer was attempting to expedite the investigation and to clear the traffic lane. Defendant persisted in standing near the police officer and arguing with him. His numerous orders for her to return to her vehicle related to his duties to control, direct, and regulate traffic. Defendant was aware of the orders and consciously chose to disobey.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed defendant's conviction.

 

1198 of 1650 DOCUMENTS


State v. Frink


CA No. A22050


COURT OF APPEALS OF OREGON


60 Ore. App. 209; 653 P.2d 553; 1982 Ore. App. LEXIS 3803


December 18, 1981, Argued and submitted  
November 10, 1982 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from District Court, Lane County. Gregory G. Foote, Judge. No. B 61-049.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The State sought review of a judgment of the District Court, Lane County (Oregon), which sustained defendant's demurrer to an indictment charging him of furnishing a photograph of a portion of the human body depicting nudity to three minors in violation of Or. Rev. Stat. § 167.065(1)(a).


OVERVIEW: Defendant contended that the state statute violated the First Amendment and Or. Const. art. I, § 8 in that the statute was an overbroad law. Pursuant to the First Amendment and the state constitutional provision, no law was to be passed restraining the free expression of opinion or restricting the right to speak freely upon any subject. The court determined that the mere depiction of nudity could not have been prohibited under the First Amendment. The court held that a state was permitted to adopt more stringent controls on communicative materials available to youths than those available to adults. However, the court found that the guidelines for judging obscenity were: (1) whether the average person, applying contemporary community standards, would have found that the work, taken as a whole, appealed to the prurient interest; (2) whether the work depicted, in a patently offensive way, sexual conduct specifically defined by the applicable state law; and (3) whether the work lacked serious artistic, political, or scientific value. The court held that the scope of the state statute could not have been narrowed by judicial interpretation and that it was overbroad.


OUTCOME: The judgment was affirmed.

 

1199 of 1650 DOCUMENTS


State v. Rainey


CA No. A20105


COURT OF APPEALS OF OREGON


60 Ore. App. 302; 653 P.2d 584; 1982 Ore. App. LEXIS 3818


December 14, 1981, Argued and submitted; September 8, 1982, Resubmitted in banc  
November 10, 1982 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Reconsideration Denied January 14, 1983. Petition for Review Allowed February 1, 1983 (294 Or 491).


PRIOR HISTORY: Appeal from Circuit Court, Multnomah County. Clifford B. Olsen, Judge. No. C80-10-33816.



DISPOSITION: Reversed and remanded for new trial.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed from the judgment of the Circuit Court, Multnomah County (Oregon), which convicted him for unlawful delivery of a controlled substance in violation of Or. Rev. Stat. § 475.992.


OVERVIEW: Defendant assisted another man in taking boxes of marijuana from a truck and placing them in the vehicle of an undercover police officer. At trial, defendant contended that he did not know the contents of the boxes. Defendant asserted that the trial court erred in instructing the jury that proof of the unlawful delivery of a controlled substance was prima facie evidence of knowledge of the character of the substance delivered. The court noted that knowing delivery of a controlled substance was an essential element of the crime. The court found that the trial court's instruction that permitted the element of knowledge to be proven from evidence of delivery alone, which defendant admitted, without reference to the circumstances under which the parcel was possessed or delivered, was error. Accordingly, the judgment was reversed.


OUTCOME: The judgment was reversed and remanded for a new trial.

 

1200 of 1650 DOCUMENTS


Wright v. Associates Financial Services Co.


CA No. A21242


COURT OF APPEALS OF OREGON


59 Ore. App. 688; 651 P.2d 1368; 1982 Ore. App. LEXIS 3385


May 6, 1982, Submitted on record and briefs  
October 13, 1982 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Reconsideration Denied November 18, 1982. Petition for Review Denied January 11, 1983 (294 Or 391).


PRIOR HISTORY: Appeal from Circuit Court, Lane County. Maurice K. Merten, Judge. No. 16-80-02377.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff borrowers challenged a judgment of the Circuit Court, Lane County (Oregon), which dismissed their complaint against defendant lender in which they sought a decree declaring that a trust deed to their property could not have been foreclosed by advertisement and sale, certain provisions of the Oregon Trust Deed Act (Act) were unconstitutional, and the loan secured by the trust deed was not in default at the time of foreclosure.


OVERVIEW: The borrowers obtained a loan from the lender. The loan was secured by a trust deed, which specified that, in the event of default, if the land was not currently used for agricultural, timber, or grazing purposes, the beneficiary could elect to foreclose by advertisement and sale. The borrowers became delinquent on their loan payments. The lender notified them of its intention to proceed with a foreclosure action if the account was not made current within 30 days. The borrowers did not bring the account current. They brought an action against the lender for declaratory relief. The lender declared a default, advertised the property for sale, and sold it. The circuit court entered judgment for the lender. The borrowers appealed. The court affirmed, holding that the lender was permitted to have foreclosed the property by advertisement and sale because the land was not used for agricultural, timber, or grazing purposes at the time the trust deed was executed. The court held that the lender adequately notified the borrowers of its intent to strictly enforce the terms of the contract, and that the lender afforded the borrowers sufficient opportunity to make up the delinquency payments.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the circuit court's judgment dismissing the borrowers' complaint against the lender in which the borrower sought a decree declaring that a trust deed to their property could not have been foreclosed by advertisement and sale, certain provisions of the Act were unconstitutional, and the loan secured by the trust deed was not in default at the time of foreclosure.
